  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a83a6791-2413-4e08-b30f-87705fa057c1'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13213825054332875389">SERVANT OF THE BONES is Anne Rice's new electrifying novel, with a hero as mesmerising, seductive and ambivalent as the vampire Lestat. Azriel is a restless Jewish spirit, born almost 2500 years ago in Babylon, who can be called forth by whoever holds and understands the arcane mystery of the casket of golden bones he is tied to. Caught between heaven and earth, Azriel is </span>
  <span id="freeText13213825054332875389" style="display:none">SERVANT OF THE BONES is Anne Rice's new electrifying novel, with a hero as mesmerising, seductive and ambivalent as the vampire Lestat. Azriel is a restless Jewish spirit, born almost 2500 years ago in Babylon, who can be called forth by whoever holds and understands the arcane mystery of the casket of golden bones he is tied to. Caught between heaven and earth, Azriel is forced to bear witness to the long and troubled history of Western civilisation, from the household of an ancient Greek philosopher and the deathbed of Alexander the Great, to the Mongolian Steppes and fourteenth century Strasbourg, where Jews were made scapegoats for the Black Death. And finally in the present, he is summoned to witness and avenge a brutal murder on Fifth Avenue. The dead woman is Esther, step-daughter of Gregory Belkin, fanatical messianic leader of a worldwide cult, the Temple of the Mind. Belkin is known to be the son of Holocaust victims, but he has a secret history which binds Azriel's fate to his. SERVANT OF THE BONES is as rich and terrifying, as sensual and violent as any novel by Anne Rice - an enthralling epic which conjures up more than two thousand years of Jewish history and penetrates the unfolding mysteries of evil, redemption, life and death.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13213825054332875389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1878295" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1878295?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Irene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Irene" href="/user/show/95142-irene">Irene</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679433019" href="/book/show/518830.Servant_of_the_Bones">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1878295" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7903763097705854063">Like most of Anne Rice's later work, this book is written in a loose, breathy semisoftcore, purple overwrought style and is sprinkled with too many one sentence paragraphs. The premise of the story isn't bad and I didn't have a hard time getting through it, but it does give one that creepy squirrely feeling of having unintentionally intruded upon someone else's sexual fantasies about Antonio Banderas.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10185460184898257070">The basic idea of this story seemed to come out of left field: A charming, fairly spiritual Babylonian sacrifices himself in a beautiful ceremony for the sake of his people. He dies (very, VERY horribly), and becomes a powerful spirit in the control of whoever owns his gold-plated skeleton. It's quite strange, and I haven't been able to find which myth it's based on (seems to be a combination of the vampire and golem myths, but that's not really descriptive of the result). 99.9% of it is told in</span>
  <span id="freeText10185460184898257070" style="display:none">The basic idea of this story seemed to come out of left field: A charming, fairly spiritual Babylonian sacrifices himself in a beautiful ceremony for the sake of his people. He dies (very, VERY horribly), and becomes a powerful spirit in the control of whoever owns his gold-plated skeleton. It's quite strange, and I haven't been able to find which myth it's based on (seems to be a combination of the vampire and golem myths, but that's not really descriptive of the result). 99.9% of it is told in flashbacks, which is great since for some reason flashbacks and history lessons are usually my favorite parts of Anne Rice's books. And of course, since it's Anne Rice we're talking about, the story is beautiful and tragic, with an immortal main character who has to cope with the fact that he doesn't ever get to die and go to heaven like everybody else.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2316665697?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sud666" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sud666" href="/user/show/36890366-sud666">Sud666</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345409669" href="/book/show/1154052.Servant_of_the_Bones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=fiction-novel">fiction-novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2316665697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12608385982872943438">The story of Azriel was an interesting one. I am more used to Anne Rice's vampires, and the occasional mummy, than I am with her other works. Servant of the Bones was a solid introduction to the other supernatural works she has written. The book shines when it focuses on Azriel's past in Babylon and amongst the Jews of Strasbourg. It's the "current" time frame and it's story where the tale struggles a bit. The messianic lunatic that he fights against isn't a terrible character and the "plan" is </span>
  <span id="freeText12608385982872943438" style="display:none">The story of Azriel was an interesting one. I am more used to Anne Rice's vampires, and the occasional mummy, than I am with her other works. Servant of the Bones was a solid introduction to the other supernatural works she has written. The book shines when it focuses on Azriel's past in Babylon and amongst the Jews of Strasbourg. It's the "current" time frame and it's story where the tale struggles a bit. The messianic lunatic that he fights against isn't a terrible character and the "plan" is actually interesting, but falls short of the quality of the rest of the "historical" story.<br /><br />In the "current" timeline Azriel is helping the wife of the messianic lunatic solve her daughter's death, allegedly at the hands of members of the cult/religion. So I am supposed to feel some measure of sympathy for these two but, as usual, in the empathy/sympathy department I find myself at a loss. Take Rachel, the daughter, who "stands up for herself" by arguing with her step-father. Um ok I can see why this current generation would equate talk with action. But I'm a "deeds" kind of guy. This special snowflake goes shopping for diamonds on Fifth Ave on a weekly basis. From where does this income come? Is she a Yale grad? Does he have a job? Nope. The money comes from her "evil" step-dad. A bit rich to be funded by the very thing you decry, nu? Who knows? I'm not a hypocrite and thus am not a liberal. Also the mother- Mrs. Belkin, who for all her tragic emotions can be summed up in this one sentence- "Stupid woman marries megalomaniac, rich, prick with messianic tendencies and realizes that he is a megalomaniac, rich, prick with messianic tendencies. Huh. Amazing. Who would have thunk it? Oh well-at least he's worth billions so we will deal with "character" after I am done shopping at Yves St. Laurent. Yeah my sympathy is barely registering. In decimal points.<br /><br />But other than that? The historical background tale does indeed save the book from itself. Oh and if you are wondering-yes there is an nearly-obligatory weird, uncomfortable sex scene. It IS Anne Rice, get over it. <br /><br />So- a nice little read about something other than Vampires. If you are a Rice fan you'll like this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12608385982872943438" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer20723396&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer20723396" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17586051836672446196">This was the first Anne Rice book that I've read, so I can't say for sure if this is a typical Rice book or not. Either way, I'll give my impressions of it.<br/><br/>The book is written in first person, and therefore comes with the limitations of that type of narration. I didn't perticularly like the narrator's style - it often felt elaborate but too descriptive when I simply wanted to know what was going to happen. I was rather bored on occations too, especially at the start because I felt that it took w</span>
  <span id="freeText17586051836672446196" style="display:none">This was the first Anne Rice book that I've read, so I can't say for sure if this is a typical Rice book or not. Either way, I'll give my impressions of it.<br /><br />The book is written in first person, and therefore comes with the limitations of that type of narration. I didn't perticularly like the narrator's style - it often felt elaborate but too descriptive when I simply wanted to know what was going to happen. I was rather bored on occations too, especially at the start because I felt that it took way too long to get interesting.<br /><br />The story itself was ok but not especially thrilling. Part of the problem here was that it was too long, and the tedious story of the narrator wasn't that interesting and took up too much of the book. The actual storyline which ends the book only really develops halfway through, by which time I was really staring to get bored.<br /><br />Overall, this could have been half as long and wouldn't have lost any great detail. In fact, I could have even been two separate books, imho.<br /><br />Whether or not this is a typical Rice book, I don't know, but it hasn't really inspired me to read any more of hers sadly.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_284088035" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/284088035" />
    <a title="Michael Barnett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5265339-michael-barnett"><img alt="Michael Barnett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1302742002p2/5265339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/284088035?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Barnett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Barnett" href="/user/show/5265339-michael-barnett">Michael Barnett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer284088035" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14307668664686105637">I started reading all of Anne Rice's vampire books, then started with the Mayfair Witches, which for the most part, enjoyed thoroughly. After a while, however, I was wanting something more. After all, how many times can you re spin books with the same central theme? Then I found Servant of the Bones. I have always loved historical epics, and beginning the story in Babylon, during the Jewish captivity there, was not only brilliant, but very well written. Miss Rice has always, not only done much r</span>
  <span id="freeText14307668664686105637" style="display:none">I started reading all of Anne Rice's vampire books, then started with the Mayfair Witches, which for the most part, enjoyed thoroughly. After a while, however, I was wanting something more. After all, how many times can you re spin books with the same central theme? Then I found Servant of the Bones. I have always loved historical epics, and beginning the story in Babylon, during the Jewish captivity there, was not only brilliant, but very well written. Miss Rice has always, not only done much research for her books--including this one--but she is then able to tell a story, making the reader feel as if he is really there with the main characters. Being a Christian, when I first realized she had ventured into the land and time of Daniel, I was wondering if she would rewrite history, but she did not. Miss Rice tells the story of Azriel, a young Jew, who is there during the conquest by Cyrus the Persian. He is then betrayed by his own people for a perceived greater good, and suffers a horrible death. A Jewish witch commands his spirit to go into the bones, and from then until the present, he can only be summoned from the bones by powerful magic. This may seem like a simple story, but the central theme is can Azriel' soul ever go to heaven, since he was made this powerful spirit against his will? I highly recommend this book to anyone who enjoys historically beautiful times, and someone who roots for the underdog.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14307668664686105637" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_620477972" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/620477972" />
    <a title="laurenpie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15952859-laurenpie"><img alt="laurenpie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356993739p2/15952859.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/620477972?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="laurenpie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="laurenpie" href="/user/show/15952859-laurenpie">laurenpie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to laurenpie by: </span>
      Shawn
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15952859-laurenpie?shelf=fantasy-mythic-creatures">fantasy-mythic-creatures</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer620477972" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14481368240448025793"><b>The <i>bones</i> of a truly fascinating literary novel, but regrettably fleshed out into a light and mediocre quick-read</b><br/><br/>I find the topic fascinating to contemplate: a millenia-old spirit, incredibly powerful, yet confused regarding his own provenance, and unsure even what he is: Angel of Wrath? Demon?<br/><br/>Knowing that he is brimming with hate and violence, yet catching fleeting glimpses of a way he once learned from a forgotten master he once loved, a way dedicated to goodness.<br/><br/>With a more diligent author, w</span>
  <span id="freeText14481368240448025793" style="display:none"><b>The <i>bones</i> of a truly fascinating literary novel, but regrettably fleshed out into a light and mediocre quick-read</b><br /><br />I find the topic fascinating to contemplate: a millenia-old spirit, incredibly powerful, yet confused regarding his own provenance, and unsure even what he is: Angel of Wrath? Demon?<br /><br />Knowing that he is brimming with hate and violence, yet catching fleeting glimpses of a way he once learned from a forgotten master he once loved, a way dedicated to goodness.<br /><br />With a more diligent author, we could have taken advantage of this brilliant and unique idea for a main character. We could have traveled through time with Azriel as he slowly recovered his memories of various masters throughout cultures and centuries, from ancient to modern day. The few chapters dedicated to this were by far the best in the novel. I would have relished experiencing the varying attitudes and philosophies through the ages, deliberating on their effect and values.<br /><br />I also wish we had spent more time delving into <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Azriel breaking his own bonds: coming to the realization that he didn't have to answer to any "master" that called him, that in fact he could wreak destruction on any who even made the attempt; eventually finding the power to call and command himself of his own free will. And finally, at long last, starting to wonder if he might, ironically, find true fulfillment and redemption in submitting himself and his hard-won freedom in service to the one true God. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I don't mean to hint that this is a religious novel... it's NOT. It's fiction, not Biblical-based at all, and not intended to be so.<br /><br />Interesting side-characters too: Cyrus, Zurvan, Samuel, the Rebbe. Though, I didn't care for Jonathan; I felt he unwarrantably intruded a little too much into the story. The complex relationship (especially that last conversation) between Samuel and Azriel was great! The plot-line behind the Temple of the Mind of God was fairly interesting. And, a small thing: it was fun reading about the compilation of warnings and safeguards that successive generations had added to the casket of bones. Right down to iron chains! LOL!<br /><br />On the downside: the constant sophomoric obsession with looks was silly (from Jonathan to Azriel to Gregory Belkin), as though Ms Rice was having trouble remembering to switch gears from writing erotica or romance. I do respect Ann Rice for her wonderful-written vampire series, and boy can she think up some interesting plots and details. However, I'm afraid she could have done better here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14481368240448025793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3617423257448103593" style="display:none">Better than expected - after struggling through the VIOLIN and that awful one about the castrated singer - I did not expect much from this - but it had most of the best elements of Rice - and fewer of the worst.<br /><br />The prose was less in the way of trying to be epic poetry, and although the characters were clunky and badly written - the pseudo-historical parts were interesting enough to keep me in there. <br /><br />Seems like this was the second straight book of Anne Rice that had the line, "I was amazed, but not surprised" Just hits the ear wrong, and then to see it in back to back books was almost comical - but not funny.<br /><br />Overall the story of the 2500 year old disembodied spirit and his pointless quest was pretty mediocre.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tassie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tassie" href="/user/show/1953488-tassie">Tassie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1953488-tassie?shelf=library">library</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer870532756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4562393325804047972">I have a love/hate relationship with Anne Rice. I love her when she moves stories along. Her skills are wonderful and drive the characters and the situations beautifully.<br/><br/>Then she lapses into feelings and souls and a bunch of mumbo-jumbo blah blah blah that I couldn't care less about.<br/><br/>This was the latter rather than the former.<br/><br/>Really, the book could have been 100 pages long, but instead takes you through a thousand years that mean nothing and twenty pages of meaningful stuff at the end.<br/><br/>Blah.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2791558011" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6336359119632409344">Azriel is a twenty year-old scholar of ancient languages in Babylon. He and his family copy important texts and books of the day. They sell gemstones, sculptures, and other finery. Although they are Jewish hostages, kept from their home in Jerusalem, they live well and seem happy. And then one day it all goes wrong. Azriel is chosen as a human sacrifice. He takes part in a great procession, a parade of sorts, with much fanfare. He is the center of attention and gleams. Azriel makes a deal with K</span>
  <span id="freeText6336359119632409344" style="display:none">Azriel is a twenty year-old scholar of ancient languages in Babylon. He and his family copy important texts and books of the day. They sell gemstones, sculptures, and other finery. Although they are Jewish hostages, kept from their home in Jerusalem, they live well and seem happy. And then one day it all goes wrong. Azriel is chosen as a human sacrifice. He takes part in a great procession, a parade of sorts, with much fanfare. He is the center of attention and gleams. Azriel makes a deal with King Cyrus, but he is not told of the catch. He learns this last minute to his horror. A great curse is placed on Azriel, but is it also a gift? Azriel becomes something else and it is incorrect to call him a genie, which really is a jinn, the Arabic word for demon. No, Azriel was a man, who is now a spirit, but no ordinary spirit. It is hard to describe a story like this, so I would say it is a historical occult. It is overly wordy, but it is original. In the end, Azriel becomes more alive than ever to stop death. </span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2951530-shawna-mckinnis?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79485890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15469734819763472911">The Servant of the Bones falls into the Crunchy Vegetable category. It took me quite a while to really get into the story. In my quest to own and read everything Anne Rice, however, I endured and interspersed my stints of reading Servant with nice helpings of Ice Cream books. <br/>In the Servant of the Bones you meet the spirit Azriel as he tells his tale to the Professor and Author Jonathan. Azriel’s tale is one of great sorrow, fear, and in the end, love. You look at the human condition on a scale </span>
  <span id="freeText15469734819763472911" style="display:none">The Servant of the Bones falls into the Crunchy Vegetable category. It took me quite a while to really get into the story. In my quest to own and read everything Anne Rice, however, I endured and interspersed my stints of reading Servant with nice helpings of Ice Cream books. <br />In the Servant of the Bones you meet the spirit Azriel as he tells his tale to the Professor and Author Jonathan. Azriel’s tale is one of great sorrow, fear, and in the end, love. You look at the human condition on a scale of an individual heart as well as on a global basis. Azriel’s tale spans thousands of years, but the culmination of his understanding is a modern thing with an intense climax. I hate spoilers, so I won’t give away too much, and I am sure you can find a more thorough review elsewhere on the web if that is what you crave. <br />I am very glad I persevered. When I was about halfway through, I began to fall in love with the spirit Azriel, and plowed though the rest of the book rather quickly. Rice has such a talent for character development- by the time you are through the book you feel you have truly known the people you have read. While her books on the Mayfair Witches are still my favorites, I look forward to continuing my journey though the books of Anne Rice. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15469734819763472911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_159854795" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159854795" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4787333-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1295290206p2/4787333.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/159854795?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/4787333-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer159854795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2799142730774593868">Although it has been a few years since I have read this book, it still stays with me. Anne Rice has an amazing attention to detail and the ability to really put the reader into her characters' heads. She uses flashbacks and backstory to develop her characters and give them personality. She even gives personality to characters that most others would have ignored. This layers the story and gives it life. In this book she goes into mythology and intertwines religions to create a unique story. The m</span>
  <span id="freeText2799142730774593868" style="display:none">Although it has been a few years since I have read this book, it still stays with me. Anne Rice has an amazing attention to detail and the ability to really put the reader into her characters' heads. She uses flashbacks and backstory to develop her characters and give them personality. She even gives personality to characters that most others would have ignored. This layers the story and gives it life. In this book she goes into mythology and intertwines religions to create a unique story. The main character is torn between religious views and suffers the consequences of his choices. Rice's imagination comes through in "high definition" in her writing and it makes you want to continue reading. She influences my writing because I want to be capable of creating a "high definition" story like hers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2799142730774593868" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_210614642" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/210614642" />
    <a title="Lizann Dennis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6126768-lizann-dennis"><img alt="Lizann Dennis" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/210614642?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lizann Dennis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lizann Dennis" href="/user/show/6126768-lizann-dennis">Lizann Dennis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer210614642" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11136522411558763790">Anne Rice tells the story slowly; A demon or god recalling his past while a temporary recluse writer transcribes the story for him is a unique story line. Rice tells the story with such care that you come to find yourself attached to this man who is now neither living or dead, no longer a man but not quite an all-powerful god. I enjoyed this strange tale of how a god is created where usually gods simply exist, they are not given a human element at all. I would like to re-read this text because I</span>
  <span id="freeText11136522411558763790" style="display:none">Anne Rice tells the story slowly; A demon or god recalling his past while a temporary recluse writer transcribes the story for him is a unique story line. Rice tells the story with such care that you come to find yourself attached to this man who is now neither living or dead, no longer a man but not quite an all-powerful god. I enjoyed this strange tale of how a god is created where usually gods simply exist, they are not given a human element at all. I would like to re-read this text because I feel there is so much more to the human side of the god presented in the writing. As for my own writing the ability of telling a story within a story would be a skill of interest. The way Rice relates it to the reader, with such smooth transitions,one has no difficulty with telling if they are in the present or the past.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11136522411558763790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer443108477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1611280363985341946">After being disappointed by 'Violin', I gave this book a try. Thankfully, it proved to be better than the other book. It's not on par with the Vampire Chronicles, but is a decent and satisfying read, whether for the Anne Rice fan or for someone just looking for a good read.<br/><br/>The story is interesting (the past story of Azriel and the present one of Belkin, his cult, and the surrounding events) and I actually liked this. I wouldn't quite give it a four, but it deserves better than 3, but unfortunate</span>
  <span id="freeText1611280363985341946" style="display:none">After being disappointed by 'Violin', I gave this book a try. Thankfully, it proved to be better than the other book. It's not on par with the Vampire Chronicles, but is a decent and satisfying read, whether for the Anne Rice fan or for someone just looking for a good read.<br /><br />The story is interesting (the past story of Azriel and the present one of Belkin, his cult, and the surrounding events) and I actually liked this. I wouldn't quite give it a four, but it deserves better than 3, but unfortunately you cannot choose half-stars. My personal rating for this book is 3.5/5 stars for a entertaining and interesting read.</span>

  <span id="freeText878903572339166066" style="display:none">When I was reading Anne Rice's "Servant of the Bones" last year, I could not decide if I cared much for her style or the story. It was not until I finished the book that I began thinking about both the story and the writing style. It took another three months before I realized how thought provoking her book had been for me. And that to me is the sign of a true artist. Since then I have enjoyed a few of her books and just finished "Interview with the Vampire" (Which I always thought was called "Interview with a Vampire".) I've learned to love her style and looking forward to reading many more of her books.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3611314021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14602240078544359125">I have a love/hate relationship with Anne Rice. I love her when she moves stories along. Her skills are wonderful and drive the characters and the situations beautifully. But sometimes she lapses into agonizingly long conversations to the point where the build up lasts so long the end gets anti-climatic.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Kathleen " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen " href="/user/show/17830025-kathleen">Kathleen </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17830025-kathleen?shelf=average-joes">average-joes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer856902958" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11837577982655243829">Okay, so it is kind of obvious that Anne Rice wrote this book right after Memnoch the Devil. Especially during Part 3 of the book. <br/>So the book starts off with Part 1, where a professor who uses the name Jonathan is approached by a genii named Azriel who would like his story known and written down. A lot of the narration up to the actual storytelling is drenched with foreshadowing, which filled me with suspense. It was confusing and surprisingly engaging. (Though Jonathan keeps telling us how ama</span>
  <span id="freeText11837577982655243829" style="display:none">Okay, so it is kind of obvious that Anne Rice wrote this book right after Memnoch the Devil. Especially during Part 3 of the book. <br />So the book starts off with Part 1, where a professor who uses the name Jonathan is approached by a genii named Azriel who would like his story known and written down. A lot of the narration up to the actual storytelling is drenched with foreshadowing, which filled me with suspense. It was confusing and surprisingly engaging. (Though Jonathan keeps telling us how amazingly gorgeous Azriel is, which was kind of annoying). <br />Azriel begins his story with how he grew up, as a boy in a Jewish family taken from Jerusalem to Babylon. The way Rice approached the culture and religion of Babylonians was well-researched and creative. Azriel sees spirits and Marduk (a spirit or real Babylonian god, it is left to interpretation) is one of them. This attracts the attention the temple priests and they want to use him as a sacrifice in a ritual that establishes the Persian conqueror, Cyrus, as the new ruler of Babylonia. <br />It is an eerie, creepy ritual that will result in Azriel's death, but he does it so all the Jews can go back to Jerusalem, which Cyrus has promised. But an evil priest and old spiritual woman hijack poor Azriel after the ritual when he is about to die and use an ancient ritual to turn him into the Servant of the Bones. Honestly, this was my favorite part of the book. It was so creepy and a little scary. <br />The next section of the book, Part 2, is about Azriel's adventures with his favorite master. He kind of briefly talks about the most recent one, which was actually my favorite single scene in the entire book. But he mostly skips over the rest of the other masters. Azriel, as a spirit, has trouble remembering his past until the very end, so we hear Azriel talking about different parts of his past while reminding us that he could't remember individual parts at the time, which was unique.<br />If the entire book was this, just Azriel's adventures as a genii in the ancient world, I would have liked it at more. Which brings me to Part 3. <br />Rice's Memnoch the Devil ideals really show through in this part. If you like Memnoch the Devil, then fine, I get it. I don't dislike it, I just see that it’s directed towards a rather narrow audience and is rather opinionated about Rice's personal religious views. This book is kind of like that, but not as much. I never quite understood Azriel's relationship with God. He seemed to believe in God as an all-powerful being, but also worshiped Marduk. But the parts about Jewish history and culture, ancient and modern, were interesting.<br />So Azriel is suddenly awakened to witness the murder of Esther Belkin. The reason for his awakening without a Master was never fully explained. Rice said on her website that it was the scene of Esther’s death that woken him. I kind of don’t buy that, but whatever. So Azriel meets Gregory Belkin and his grandfather discussing Esther’s death and the Servant of the Bones (the casket containing Azirel’s bones has been gifted down to the grandfather). The grandfather hates Gregory and gives him the cursed casket. <br />Gregory Belkin is the leader of The Temple of the Mind of God, a weird, wide-spread Jewish cult. Apparently he is incredibly rich from his career as a televangelist. (Rice wrote that Dora, the woman in her last book, Memnoch, was a televangelist too). <br />Azriel confronts Gregory and seems to simultaneously despise and love him, which wasn’t confusing at all. He sees Rachel, Gregory’s wife, mourning for Esther. Gregory is apparently evil and unkind to his wife and hired the killers that murdered Esther. So Azriel helps Rachel, who is dying of a disease, leave to die alone with him in Miami. <br />They have sex and Gregory and his henchmen find them, but not before Rachel kills herself. Gregory reveals that he has a crazy plan to take over the world and kill everyone undesirable in the Third World and other places. This plan was only revealed until about 30 pages before the book ended, so it felt kind of abrupt. Part 4 is the epilogue, more or less.<br />The amount of thought Rice put into Azriel’s powers and origin story was very impressive. It was entertaining during the first half, but I kind of had to force myself to read the second. She should have stuck to feel and set-up of the first half, but it was still an incredibly cool twist about the origins of genies.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11837577982655243829" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2663556118" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2663556118" />
    <a title="Whiskey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79191615-whiskey"><img alt="Whiskey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521523346p2/79191615.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2663556118?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whiskey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whiskey" href="/user/show/79191615-whiskey">Whiskey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79191615-whiskey?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/79191615-whiskey?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2663556118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15946221255216125312"><i>"There is one purpose to life: to bear witness to and understand as much as possible of the complexity of the world—its beauty, its mysteries, its riddles. The more you understand, the more you look, the greater is your enjoyment of life and your sense of peace. Always, if you have a choice, be kind. Remember the poor, the hungry, and the miserable. Always remember the suffering and those who need. The greatest creative power you have on earth is to help others-the poor the hungry, the oppressed</i></span>
  <span id="freeText15946221255216125312" style="display:none"><i>"There is one purpose to life: to bear witness to and understand as much as possible of the complexity of the world—its beauty, its mysteries, its riddles. The more you understand, the more you look, the greater is your enjoyment of life and your sense of peace. Always, if you have a choice, be kind. Remember the poor, the hungry, and the miserable. Always remember the suffering and those who need. The greatest creative power you have on earth is to help others-the poor the hungry, the oppressed. To ease pain and give joy are your finest powers. Kindness is a human miracle, so to speak. It’s unique to us humans, and our more developed angels or spirits, to be kind. To love and to learn that is the purpose of life."</i><br /><br />Azriel was born a Hebrew in Babylon during the captivity of the Israelites under King Nabonidus. By firelight, The Servant of the Bones tells his story to Jonathan Ben Issac, a college professor with Jewish lineage. As a young man, Azriel worked as a scribe in the temple of Marduk. Throughout the novel, Azriel struggles to understand whether he is a ghost, a demon, or an angel. He tries to understand why God has denied him the Stairway to Heaven by allowing him to be made into an immortal spirit who is bound to the gold-encased bones of his mortal body. As a genie, he must obey the Master of those bones (whoever has them at the moment) and become the Master's Servant, whether for good or evil. Thus the title, Servant of the Bones.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ The Babylonian priests observe that Azriel seems to have a special rapport with the god. Azriel, despite being a Jew, has chosen Marduk as his personal god, and he talks, prays, and makes offerings to him. Azriel's father, with whom he is very close, treats this lightly, but none of Azriel's family realizes that Marduk actually talks back to him. At nineteen, Azriel becomes strong enough to make the god visible, to even touch him and walk with him. This brings Azriel the unwanted notice of the prophet Enoch, the witch Asenath, and Remath and the priests of Marduk.<br /><br />Cyrus the Persian has conquered Babylon, and he wants a peaceful transition of power. The priests of Marduk have a ceremony in which the statue of Marduk tours the city with the king to show his favor. The statue is made of a human body coated in gold, and the old statue has rotted through.<br /><br />Azriel, who bears a striking resemblance to the god and is clearly favored by him, is asked to become the new statue. He is coated in a special, poisonous mixture of gold and tours the city, a living representative of the god, showing his people that Cyrus is to be their new king. In exchange for his life, Azriel buys the freedom of the Israelites. At the end of the ceremony, however, Azriel is tricked by Remath and Asenath. He is thrown into a pot of boiling gold, and only his bones remain; his spirit is then bound, genie-like, to the bones. Azriel calls for Marduk's help. The god turns away, however, and Azriel's spirit is driven into the bones.<br /><br />As The Servant of the Bones, Azriel is called upon over the centuries by many a powerful wizard. The first, Zurvan, teaches him the nature of magic, and the idea that the Hebrew God is the same as all other major gods such as Zeus and Ahuramazda.<br /><br />After being betrayed by one master, Azriel begins to kill all wizards who call him out of the bones, until finally, he appears unbidden on the streets of modern New York in time to witness the murder of Rachel Belkin, daughter of Gregory Belkin, the leader of the cultish Temple of the Mind. He also explores the relationship between faith and fanatical madness. The Temple of the Mind, while bringing happiness and enlightenment to its millions of followers, is at its heart more corrupt and destructive than any cult. <br /><br />A Hasidic prodigy, Gregory Belkin, turns against his faith and heritage, choosing instead to found a cult to redirect people's faith in God to himself. With cold calculation, he plots the "assassinations" of his stepdaughter, Rachel, and his twin brother Nathan, and through Nathan's death, he mimics the resurrection of Christ, transforming himself into a god. Azriel represents Belkin's ultimate temptation: to become a godlike spirit, a creature beyond earthly concerns. It is this ravaging spirit that the witch Asenath would have wanted to become the Servant of the Bones, a creature turned away from God and controllable in its anger and vanity.<br /><br />Characters<br />Azriel: formerly a mortal young man of Babylon in 539 BC, now a spirit bound to obey whoever possesses his gold-encased mortal bones.<br />Jonathan Ben Isaac: the history professor whom Azriel seeks to hear and write his tale.<br />In Babylon<br />Aseneth: the witch who betrays Azriel and kills him in order to turn him into the Servant of the Bones. She dies before she is able to complete the ritual.<br />Cyrus the Great or Cyrus the Persian: the man who conquered Babylon. Made a deal with Azriel that if Azriel became the new Marduk statue, all his people could return to the Holy Land and rebuild their temple.<br />Marduk: a spirit who is worshiped as a Babylonian god. He befriends the mortal Azriel and becomes his 'personal god' throughout his mortal existence. Sadly, Azriel's stunning resemblance to the statue of Marduk causes him to be chosen for the ceremony to create a new statue every one hundred years, thus inadvertently turning him into the Servant of the Bones.<br />Nabonidus: the deceased king of Babylon<br /><br />In Miletus<br />Zurvan: the magus who becomes Azriel's first master and teacher. Azriel does not remember his time with Zurvan until 2000 years has passed and he awakes in New York, Zurvan's teachings of kindness and virtues stay with him throughout the centuries.<br />In Strassbourg France<br />Samuel: a wealthy Jewish merchant. He made a deal with some Gentiles that he would give Azriel's bones to them to do what he pleased if they would bring his daughters to safety. Whether Azriel truly loved him in the end or not is a matter of debate, because the scene begins with Azriel begging Samuel to fly him away from the oncoming pogrom, but ends on a sad note with Azriel being forced back into the bones cursing his master for being a martyr. This begins Azriel's onslaught of all his subsequent masters.<br />In New York City<br />Avram the Rebbe: Gregory's estranged Grandfather; a Jewish holy man and keeper of the Bones (although he never called upon Azriel).<br />Esther Belkin: the girl who was assassinated on Gregory—her stepfather's—orders. Azriel is there to witness this, and for reasons he can't explain wants to get revenge for the girl he knew for only a few seconds.<br />Gregory Belkin: founder and leader of the Temple of the Mind of God. Also owns a considerable commercial and merchanting empire. Uses Esther as a ploy to begin a worldwide genocide to bring world peace, with his Temple as the new world government. Although he likens himself to be the Messiah, Azriel groups him with Hitler and Stalin, saying he is an insane murderer. His idol is Alexander the Great.<br />Rachel Belkin: Gregory's wife; dying of a terminal illness, she claims Gregory is poisoning her and begs Azriel to fly with her to Miami so she may die in her own home. Last few minutes of her life are spent having intercourse with Azriel.<br />Billy Joel Eval: Eldest of the three Eval brothers who are the paid assassins of Esther.<br />Nathan: Gregory's estranged identical twin is a diamond merchant in New York City; he met Esther by chance and told her of his familial connection to her stepfather, Gregory.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Azriel is an avenging angel made flesh once again. His faith and the testing of that faith comprise his sole motivation. At each step, he tries to determine what God would want him to do, what more God requires of him, and what steps he must take to reach heaven. As a metaphor, however, Azriel illustrates the childlike ideal of faith. He accepts instruction from his masters, and when they betray him, he waits for signs from Yahweh himself. Through his faith, he is transformed, freed of the need for a master and from dependence on the Bones. He is sustained by water and his faith in God alone. Throughout the novel, Rice speculates on the nature of God and Heaven. Azriel himself undergoes the realization that, while he was intended to be a spirit of great evil, he has instead the potential for great good. <br /><br />I most enjoyed the evolution of Azriel as he tries to follow the teachings of Zurvan, the Magi from Milos, who instructs that observation, curiosity, kindness, and love make us divine.<i>“Kindness is a human miracle, so to speak. It's unique to us humans, and our more developed angels or spirits, to be kind.”</i>Rice's trademark is her sensual writing style. Her rhythmic, lyrical style and the use of Hebrew ease the narrative along. Rice joins together liturgical beliefs and scientific fact. Biblical and historical references give a strong mystical verisimilitude. I particularly enjoyed the early sections in Babylon and the Greek port of Miletus, but thought the narrative dragged somewhat in the New York section before ramping up again as the climax neared.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15946221255216125312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_586313068" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/586313068" />
    <a title="Angelica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18378839-angelica"><img alt="Angelica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363322629p2/18378839.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/586313068?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angelica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angelica" href="/user/show/18378839-angelica">Angelica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer586313068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17920913344445869802">75. SERVANT OF THE BONES, BY ANNE RICE<br/><br/>Another recommendation from Michelle. She just said “Anne Rice”, I chose this one because it was a standalone and it had been in my to-read pile for ages. Once I started reading, I got the distinct impression that I had already read it… I didn’t remember anything of the story, but there were some particular phrases that I’m sure I had read before. I really don’t think this book was at all forgettable, but I’m probably lying, since apparently I did forget abo</span>
  <span id="freeText17920913344445869802" style="display:none">75. SERVANT OF THE BONES, BY ANNE RICE<br /><br />Another recommendation from Michelle. She just said “Anne Rice”, I chose this one because it was a standalone and it had been in my to-read pile for ages. Once I started reading, I got the distinct impression that I had already read it… I didn’t remember anything of the story, but there were some particular phrases that I’m sure I had read before. I really don’t think this book was at all forgettable, but I’m probably lying, since apparently I did forget about it once already.<br /><br />Synopsis: Azriel was a Jew living in the Babylonian empire until, after a double betrayal, he is sacrificed and incompetently transformed into a spirit who can be called forth by someone who has the knowledge necessary and the possession of his bones. He exists like that for many years, unable to remember his own history, until finally he is called and witnesses the murder of a woman. This involves him in the plans of Gregory Belkin, leader of a strange worldwide cult with a dangerous thirst for power.<br /><br />Overall enjoyment: Like I said, I quite liked it. It was very imaginative, if not particularly well-written. (It wasn’t badly written either, just not especially developed)<br /><br />Plot: This book is told as an interview, very similar to Interview with the Vampire. It has two distinct parts: in the beginning Azriel relates his own history, how he came to be and a resume of the masters he’s had since he became a spirit. In the second part, he tells the story of Gregory Belkin, how he became involved with him and his plans and what happened afterwards. There is a very clear conflict of tone between those two parts. They’re very different, and I’ve noticed that there is a tendency for people to like either one or the other. The transition between them could have been better done, and she could have linked them better. As it is, the second part, which has much more action and suspense, feels unnecessary and contrite.<br /><br />Characters: Competently made, if not particularly worthy of note. Azriel is fascinating, all the others are very much secondary. As in all of the books by Rice that I’ve read, she puts way too much stock on physical appearance. Everyone has to be gorgeous. Now that I’m not a teenager anymore, I have to say that those endless and highly lyrical descriptions can get boring after a while.<br /><br />World/setting: She does a very good job on transporting us to different periods of time as Azriel tells his story. By comparison, the second part, which is set on the (somewhat) present age, feels pale and uninteresting. <br /><br />Writing style: Straightforward and easy to read.<br /><br />Representation: This book has the homoerotic undertones that I’ve always loved in her writing. It is only an undertone, though, nothing explicit. Also, the last book by her I’ve read was quite a while ago (more than 10 years, I think), before I discovered feminism, and now I was shocked to see how few female characters she has. Plus, since I mentioned all her characters being gorgeous, I distinctly remember being 11 or so, reading one of her vampire novels, and concluding I would never get to become a vampire because I wasn’t pretty enough…<br /><br />Political correctness: …which would lead me to conclude, now, that she does put way too much value on physical appearance. And her female characters are so secondary as to barely exist. They both die, one of them immediately, which fuels Azriel to find out who were her killers and why she was killed (refrigerator, anyone?) and the other one right after having sex with Azriel (for the first time without a master) and teaching him how to feel (ugh). Now that I think of it, this book (and probably her other books too, but I couldn’t say for sure since it’s been so long since I’ve read them) feels a lot like 90s fanfiction: lots of gay romance and attraction, but you’re not supposed to put women on the stories at all.<br /><br />Up next: When Will There Be Good News?, by Kate Atkinson</span>
  <a data-text-id="17920913344445869802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1613465617" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1613465617" />
    <a title="Lindsay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2542928-lindsay"><img alt="Lindsay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1441306520p2/2542928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1613465617?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/2542928-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780345389411" href="/book/show/159543.Servant_of_the_Bones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2542928-lindsay?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2542928-lindsay?shelf=sci-fi-fantasy">sci-fi-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2542928-lindsay?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2542928-lindsay?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1613465617" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11151447135034166001">This book makes a lot of poor choices that turn an epic tale of an undead scholar from ancient Babylon into an eye-roll-worthy yawnfest. Taking a cue from Interview with a Vampire (which I adore), this book is a story within a story, where a paranormal creature relates the story of its life to another person, who is tasked with telling the world. The problem with this iteration is that the man who receives the tale is a boring and unrelatable old professor. He meets the ghostly Servant of the Bo</span>
  <span id="freeText11151447135034166001" style="display:none">This book makes a lot of poor choices that turn an epic tale of an undead scholar from ancient Babylon into an eye-roll-worthy yawnfest. Taking a cue from Interview with a Vampire (which I adore), this book is a story within a story, where a paranormal creature relates the story of its life to another person, who is tasked with telling the world. The problem with this iteration is that the man who receives the tale is a boring and unrelatable old professor. He meets the ghostly Servant of the Bones, Azriel, while deathly ill after having inadvertently stranded himself at his snowy mountain retreat while finishing his latest history book. He is prone to fits of overindulgent description, and he goes on constantly about how attractive Azriel is despite the fact that HE IS NOT GAY NO SIR. Yeah, tell it to the judge, mister.<br /><br />I found the tale of Azriel’s relationship with his patron Babylonian god, Marduk, fairly compelling, and I would have liked to have heard more. Instead, we hear about the Jews who are exiled to Babylon, and they’re frankly all asshats. If I had been Azriel, I would have left my Jewish family, who clearly don’t love me if they’re willing to <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ offer me as a human sacrifice  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> in return for getting into Cyrus’s good graces. If anyone tells you <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ getting coated in poison-laced gold and being pitched into a boiling cauldron  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> is love, run—do not walk!—the opposite direction.<br /><br />The story happening in the present time (mid-1990’s) involving the Temple of the Mind is uninventive. Paranormal murder mystery? Been there, done that. And though I accept the possibility that the ideas were fresh when this book was published, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ man-made epidemics  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and religious cults are really played out. Given Azriel’s ability to change his appearance that he demonstrates at the beginning of the book, it was easy to figure out how the book was going to end. His subsequent, Interview-with-a-Vampire-esque Louis-and-Armand-style <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ destinationless wanderings  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> were a letdown.<br /><br />And one more thing: what’s with the sex with the <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ cult leader’s dying wife  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>? I mean, she has <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ end-stage cancer  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> or something, and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ is just about ready to keel over on the spot  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, and the reaction is to bone her? Seriously unnecessary and frankly a little gross. I honestly couldn’t read it. The part about how the <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ doctors doing the autopsy found his semen inside her  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> made me gag. Massive DO NOT WANT.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11151447135034166001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_108408483" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/108408483" />
    <a title="Maja Helena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3913963-maja-helena"><img alt="Maja Helena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567520726p2/3913963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/108408483?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maja Helena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maja Helena" href="/user/show/3913963-maja-helena">Maja Helena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099184423" href="/book/show/1154050.Servant_Of_The_Bones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3913963-maja-helena?shelf=in-danish">in-danish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3913963-maja-helena?shelf=paranormal-or-urban-fantasy">paranormal-or-urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3913963-maja-helena?shelf=adult-or-new-adult">adult-or-new-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3913963-maja-helena?shelf=unhauled">unhauled</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer108408483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17236228222795854669">Actual rating: 2.75<br/><br/>It was better than I thought! In a way, I grew up with Anne Rice. My mom loved her books in her <i>Vampire Chronicles</i> series and talked about them every chance she got. I practically knew the plot of <i>Interview with the Vampire</i> and <i>The Queen of the Damned</i> several years before I actually read them! I loved her <i>Vampire Chronicles</i> and really enjoyed her series on the Mayfair witches but I haven’t had the same love for her other books.<br/><br/> I still enjoy her books but not as much as I used</span>
  <span id="freeText17236228222795854669" style="display:none">Actual rating: 2.75<br /><br />It was better than I thought! In a way, I grew up with Anne Rice. My mom loved her books in her <i>Vampire Chronicles</i> series and talked about them every chance she got. I practically knew the plot of <i>Interview with the Vampire</i> and <i>The Queen of the Damned</i> several years before I actually read them! I loved her <i>Vampire Chronicles</i> and really enjoyed her series on the Mayfair witches but I haven’t had the same love for her other books.<br /><br /> I still enjoy her books but not as much as I used to, I fear.<br /><br />This book follows the same recipe as <i>Interview with the Vampire</i>; an ancient supernatural being seeks out someone he can tell his life story to. In this book, we follow the spirit Azriel who was more or less tricked into sacrificing himself and thus becomes servant of the bones. This book is not only about Azriel’s beginning but also about his most recent encounter with the world in the form of Gregory Belkin, a leader of a religious cult, and his plans to walk in the footsteps of Alexander the Great. <br /><br />At times, this book felt a bit all over the place and it could have been a lot shorter if the writing had been more focused but after having read several Anne Rice books, I know this is not her style; her writing is all over the place and she tends of go off in one direction while the plot moves in a second. This writing style seems to have been a better fit with the teenage-me instead of the 20-something-me. <br /><br />I liked this book just fine and, as I said, found it much better than I thought. Azriel’s story is at times very interesting and I enjoyed the plot with Gregory and his cult. Sadly, I found a lot of potential in regards to Azriel and his character was simply wasted – so much could have been explored but we never got in depth with any of it - and I’ve read books that incorporated the ‘mad cult leader’ plot better than it was here. It all just felt a bit…. Shallow, I guess.<br /><br />I think I’ll return to her <i>Vampire Chronicles</i> and see if I still love those!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17236228222795854669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iANdBBoPKPIB3U+YaWYVS8iCSeMsJu7DHXtInT6ahJ5YgoxTKtigDUAS5s4zaL2TcbuEOvFWWr+VpISFTXBDqA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hi3nCcSOMGrDvJ6vloPo3EF/ll7nc4flimXOl09aXT5WrDZe9Fm4lYJzN/nMjUAE+EZbhzoDM5kCugKPPLCaCA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

That the books are entertainment seems a given, and for that I\'m grateful. That they can be s<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3820823064137279494\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;The meaning of The Vampire Chronicles is still unfolding for me, and is as much connected to God as to the Devil, as much connected to pleasure as to pain. That the books are entertainment seems a given, and for that I\'m grateful. That they can be seen as a religious journey is also clear. I am immersed in the questions, and praying for the answers. Lestat, c\'est moi. I want you to love all of my characters. I am, in the writing of these and other novels, as ambitious as Dickens. I want everybody to cry when Claudia dies, just like they did for Little Nell.&quot;-- Anne RiceFebruary 8, 1995Interview with the Vampire. The Vampire Lestat. The Queen of the Damned. The Talc of the Body Thief Memnoch the Devil. With her bestselling, epic saga, Anne Rice has reinvented the legend and literature of one of fiction\'s most popular and captivating archetypes. Now, Katherine Ramsland, Anne Rice\'s biographer, has completely revised and updated the ultimate reference guide to the world, history, and adventures of these haunting characters, from the unforgettable Lestat to Rice\'s newest chilling creation, Memnoch the Devil.Written with the full cooperation of Anne Rice -- and now with more than 1,200 entries -- The Vampire Companion offers an insightful exploration, appreciation, and interpretation of all the characters and events, names and places, symbols and themes in the five volumes of The Vampire Chronicles, including more than one hundred pages of new entries on Rice\'s latest vampire novel, Memnoch the Devil. Everything that readers might want to explore further is annotated and analyzed in this entertaining and enlightening work: the unforgettable characters of Louis, Lestat, Claudia, Akasha, Armand, and Memnoch; the important points of interest on Lestat\'s fascinating tour through Heaven and Hell; ancient lore; Rice\'s unique contributions to the mythos of vampires; the literary inspirations that echo through the novels; Rice\'s own reflections and revelations about her work and about the movie Interview with the Vampire; and much more.Richly illustrated with new photos, drawings, maps, and a complete time line of events in The Chronicles, The Vampire Companion is as captivating as the phenomenal saga it explores. It is a guidebook that no visitor to the world of Anne Rice should be without.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3820823064137279494\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7577.Anne_Rice">About Anne Rice</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7577.Anne_Rice"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1383250078p3/7577.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7577.Anne_Rice">Anne Rice
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
21,711
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1321944022648677139">Anne Rice (born Howard Allen Frances O'Brien) is a best-selling American author of gothic, supernatural, historical, erotica, and later religious themed books. Best known for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/search?utf8=%E2%9C%93&query=The+Vampire+Chronicles' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Vampire Chronicles</a>, her prevailing thematical focus is on love, death, immortality, existentialism, and the human condition. She was married to poet <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/222961.Stan_Rice' title='Stan Rice' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stan Rice</a> for 41 years until his death in 2002. Her books have sold near</span>
  <span id="freeText1321944022648677139" style="display:none">Anne Rice (born Howard Allen Frances O'Brien) is a best-selling American author of gothic, supernatural, historical, erotica, and later religious themed books. Best known for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search?utf8=%E2%9C%93&amp;query=The+Vampire+Chronicles" rel="nofollow noopener">The Vampire Chronicles</a>, her prevailing thematical focus is on love, death, immortality, existentialism, and the human condition. She was married to poet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/222961.Stan_Rice" title="Stan Rice" rel="nofollow noopener">Stan Rice</a> for 41 years until his death in 2002. Her books have sold nearly 100 million copies, making her one of the most widely read authors in modern history.<br /><br />She uses the pseudonym <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17553.Anne_Rampling" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne Rampling</a> for adult-themed fiction (i.e., erotica) and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/437669.A_N_Roquelaure" rel="nofollow noopener">A.N. Roquelaure</a> for fiction featuring sexually explicit sado-masochism.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1321944022648677139" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
